style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pioneer_9" title="Pioneer 9">Pioneer 11</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_at_Jupiter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="1360" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Pioneer_10_at_Jupiter.jpg/220px-Pioneer_10_at_Jupiter.jpg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Pioneer_10_at_Jupiter.jpg/440px-Pioneer_10_at_Jupiter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_at_Jupiter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s impression of <i>Pioneer 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s flyby of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a></div></div></div>\n<p><i><b>Pioneer 10</b></i> (originally designated <b>Pioneer F</b>) is an American <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a>, launched in 1972 and weighing 258 <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilograms</a> (569 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pounds</a>), that completed the first mission to the planet <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup> Thereafter, <i>Pioneer 10</i> became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_leaving_the_Solar_System" title="List of artificial objects leaving the Solar System">first of five artificial objects</a> to achieve the <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> that will allow them to <a href="/wiki/Solar_System#Farthest_regions" title="Solar System">leave the Solar System</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a> project was conducted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Ames_Research_Center" title="NASA Ames Research Center">NASA Ames Research Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, and the space probe was manufactured by <a href="/wiki/TRW_Inc." title="TRW Inc.">TRW Inc.</a>\n</p><p><i>Pioneer 10</i> was assembled around a hexagonal <a href="/wiki/Satellite_bus" title="Satellite bus">bus</a> with a 2.74-meter (9&#160;ft 0&#160;in) diameter parabolic dish high-gain antenna, and the spacecraft was <a href="/wiki/Spin-stabilisation" title="Spin-stabilisation">spin stabilized</a> around the axis of the antenna. Its <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a> was supplied by four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generators" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generators">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> that provided a combined 155 watts at launch.\n</p><p>It was launched on March 2, 1972, by an Atlas-Centaur expendable vehicle from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_36" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 36">Cape Canaveral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. Between July 15, 1972, and February 15, 1973, it became the first spacecraft to traverse the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt#Exploration" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>. Photography of Jupiter began November 6, 1973, at a range of 25,000,000 kilometers (16,000,000&#160;mi), and about 500 images were transmitted. The closest approach to the planet was on December 4, 1973, at a range of 132,252 kilometers (82,178&#160;mi). During the mission, the on-board instruments were used to study the asteroid belt, the environment around Jupiter, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, and eventually the far reaches of the Solar System and <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_communications" title="Radio communications">Radio communications</a> were lost with <i>Pioneer 10</i> on January 23, 2003, because of the loss of electric power for its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_transmitter" title="Radio transmitter">radio transmitter</a>, with the probe at a distance of 12&#160;billion kilometers (80&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>) from Earth.\n</p>\n<div media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_background">Mission background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_Construction.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="626" data-file-width="488" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pioneer_10_Construction.jpg/200px-Pioneer_10_Construction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pioneer_10_Construction.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10_Construction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pioneer_10_Construction.jpg/400px-Pioneer_10_Construction.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Pioneer 10</i> in the final stages of construction</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_on_its_kickmotor.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1974" data-file-width="1934" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pioneer_10_on_its_kickmotor.jpg/200px-Pioneer_10_on_its_kickmotor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pioneer_10_on_its_kickmotor.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10_on_its_kickmotor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pioneer_10_on_its_kickmotor.jpg/400px-Pioneer_10_on_its_kickmotor.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Pioneer 10</i> on a Star-37E kick motor just prior to being encapsulated for launch</div></div></div></div>\n<p>In the 1960s, American aerospace engineer <a href="/wiki/Gary_Flandro" title="Gary Flandro">Gary Flandro</a> of the NASA <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a> conceived of a mission, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_Grand_Tour" title="Planetary Grand Tour">Planetary Grand Tour</a>, that would exploit a rare alignment of the outer planets of the Solar System. This mission would ultimately be accomplished in the late 1970s by the two <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> probes, but in order to prepare for it, NASA decided in 1964 to experiment with launching a pair of probes to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outer_Solar_System" title="Outer Solar System">outer Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaunius200436_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaunius200436-2">[2]</a></sup> An advocacy group named the Outer Space Panel and chaired by American space scientist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_A._Van_Allen" title="James A. Van Allen">James A. Van Allen</a>, worked out the scientific rationale for exploring the outer planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Allen2001155_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Allen2001155-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows199016_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows199016-4">[4]</a></sup> NASA <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard</a> Spaceflight Center put together a proposal for a pair of "Galactic Jupiter Probes" that would pass through the asteroid belt and visit Jupiter. These were to be launched in 1972 and 1973 during favorable windows that occurred only a few weeks every 13 months. Launch during other time intervals would have been more costly in terms of propellant requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows1999476_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows1999476-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Approved by NASA in February 1969,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows1999476_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows1999476-5">[5]</a></sup> the twin spacecraft were designated Pioneer F and Pioneer G before launch; later they were named <i>Pioneer 10</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i>. They formed part of the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneer program</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurgess198216_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurgess198216-6">[6]</a></sup> a series of United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unmanned_space_missions" title="Unmanned space missions">unmanned space missions</a> launched between 1958 and 1978. This model was the first in the series to be designed for exploring the outer Solar System. Based on multiple proposals issued throughout the 1960s, the early mission objectives were to explore the interplanetary medium past the orbit of Mars, study the asteroid belt and assess the possible hazard to spacecraft traveling through the belt, and explore Jupiter and its environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_sp349_396_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_sp349_396-7">[7]</a></sup> Later development-stage objectives included the probe closely approaching Jupiter to provide data on the effect the environmental radiation surrounding Jupiter would have on the spacecraft instruments.\n</p><p>More than 150 scientific experiments were proposed for the missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001144_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001144-8">[8]</a></sup> The experiments to be carried on the spacecraft were selected in a series of planning sessions during the 1960s, then were finalized by early 1970. These would be to perform imaging and <a href="/wiki/Polarimetry" title="Polarimetry">polarimetry</a> of Jupiter and several of its satellites, make infrared and ultraviolet observations of Jupiter, detect asteroids and meteoroids, determine the composition of charged particles, and to measure magnetic fields, plasma, cosmic rays and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zodiacal_Light" title="Zodiacal Light">Zodiacal Light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_sp349_396_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_sp349_396-7">[7]</a></sup> Observation of the spacecraft communications as it passed behind Jupiter would allow measurements of the planetary atmosphere, while tracking data would improve estimates of the mass of Jupiter and its moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_sp349_396_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_sp349_396-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Ames_Research_Center" title="NASA Ames Research Center">NASA Ames Research Center</a>, rather than Goddard, was selected to manage the project as part of the Pioneer program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows1999476_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows1999476-5">[5]</a></sup> The Ames Research Center, under the direction of Charles F. Hall, was chosen because of its previous experience with spin-stabilized spacecraft. The requirements called for a small, lightweight spacecraft which was magnetically clean and which could perform an interplanetary mission. It was to use spacecraft modules that had already been proven in the Pioneer 6 through 9 missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_sp349_396_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_sp349_396-7">[7]</a></sup>Ames commissioned a documentary film by George Van Valkenburg titled "Jupiter Odyssey". It received numerous international awards, and is visible on Van Valkenburg\'s YouTube channel. \n</p><p>In February 1970, Ames awarded a combined $380 million contract to TRW for building both of the <i>Pioneer 10</i> and <i>11</i> vehicles, bypassing the usual bidding process to save time. B. J. O\'Brien and Herb Lassen led the TRW team that assembled the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyer1998302_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyer1998302-9">[9]</a></sup> Design and construction of the spacecraft required an estimated 25 million man-hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolverton2004124_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolverton2004124-10">[10]</a></sup>  An engineer from TRW said "This spacecraft is guaranteed for two years of interplanetary flight. If any component fails within that warranty period, just return the spacecraft to our shop and we will repair it free of charge."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To meet the schedule, the first launch would need to take place between February 29 and March 17 so that it could arrive at Jupiter in November 1974. This was later revised to an arrival date of December 1973 in order to avoid conflicts with other missions over the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> for communications, and to miss the period when Earth and Jupiter would be at opposite sides of the Sun. The encounter trajectory for <i>Pioneer 10</i> was selected to maximize the information returned about the radiation environment around Jupiter, even if this caused damage to some systems. It would come within about three times the radius of the planet, which was thought to be the closest it could approach and still survive the radiation. The trajectory chosen would give the spacecraft a good view of the sunlit side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows199016&amp;ndash;19_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows199016&amp;ndash;19-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_design">Spacecraft design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spacecraft design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2254" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg/220px-Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg/330px-Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg/440px-Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_systems_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Pioneer 10</i> and <i>Pioneer 11</i> spacecraft diagram</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>Pioneer 10</i> bus measured 36 centimeters (14&#160;in) deep and with six 76-centimeter (30&#160;in) long panels forming the hexagonal structure. The bus housed propellant to control the orientation of the probe and eight of the eleven scientific instruments. The equipment compartment lay within an aluminum honeycomb structure to provide protection from <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a>. A layer of insulation, consisting of aluminized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mylar" title="Mylar">mylar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kapton" title="Kapton">kapton</a> blankets, provided passive thermal control. Heat was generated by the dissipation of 70 to 120 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> (W) from the electrical components inside the compartment. The heat range was maintained within the operating limits of the equipment by means of louvers located below the mounting platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd65_8_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-prd65_8-13">[13]</a></sup> The spacecraft had a launch mass of about 260 kilograms (570&#160;lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>42</span></sup>\n</p><p>At launch, the spacecraft carried 36 kilograms (79&#160;lb) of liquid <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">monopropellant</a> in a 42-centimeter (17&#160;in) diameter spherical tank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd65_8_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-prd65_8-13">[13]</a></sup> Orientation of the spacecraft was maintained with six 4.5 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astronautix_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astronautix-14">[14]</a></sup> hydrazine thrusters mounted in three pairs. Pair one maintained a constant spin-rate of 4.8 <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a>, pair two controlled the forward thrust, and pair three controlled the attitude. The attitude pair were used in <a href="/wiki/Conical_scanning" title="Conical scanning">conical scanning</a> maneuvers to track Earth in its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Orientation information was also provided by a <a href="/wiki/Star_tracker#Star_tracker" title="Star tracker">star sensor</a> able to reference <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/Sun_sensor" title="Sun sensor">Sun sensors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198046&#8211;47_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198046&#8211;47-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_and_communications">Power and communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Power and communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="197" data-file-width="233" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg/200px-Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Two of the SNAP-19 RTGs mounted on an extension boom</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_11_-_p42.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="175" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Pioneer_11_-_p42.jpg" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Testing spin rotation centered along the main communication dish axis</div></div></div></div>\n<p><i>Pioneer 10</i> used four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SNAP-19" title="SNAP-19">SNAP-19</a> <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> (RTGs). They were positioned on two three-rod trusses, each 3 meters (9.8&#160;ft) in length and 120 degrees apart. This was expected to be a safe distance from the sensitive scientific experiments carried on board. Combined, the RTGs provided 155&#160;W at launch, and decayed to 140&#160;W in transit to Jupiter. The spacecraft required 100&#160;W to power all systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>44&#8211;45</span></sup> The generators were powered by the radioisotope fuel <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a>, which was housed in a multi-layer capsule protected by a graphite heat shield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skrabek_mcgrew1987_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-skrabek_mcgrew1987-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The pre-launch requirement for the SNAP-19 was to provide power for two years in space; this was greatly exceeded during the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bennett_skrabek1996_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-bennett_skrabek1996-18">[18]</a></sup> The plutonium-238 has a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 87.74 years, so that after 29 years the radiation being generated by the RTGs was at 80% of its intensity at launch. However, steady deterioration of the <a href="/wiki/Thermocouple" title="Thermocouple">thermocouple</a> junctions led to a more rapid decay in electrical power generation, and by 2001 the total power output was 65&#160;W. As a result, later in the mission only selected instruments could be operated at any one time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd65_8_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-prd65_8-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The space probe included a redundant system of <a href="/wiki/Transceiver" title="Transceiver">transceivers</a>, one attached to the narrow-beam, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a>, the other to an omni-antenna and medium-gain antenna. The parabolic dish for the high-gain antenna was 2.74 meters (9.0&#160;ft) in diameter and made from an aluminum honeycomb sandwich material. The spacecraft was spun  about an axis that was parallel to the axis of this antenna so that it could remain oriented toward the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd65_8_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-prd65_8-13">[13]</a></sup> Each transceiver was 8&#160;W and transmitted data across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-band" title="S-band">S-band</a> using 2110&#160;MHz for the uplink from Earth and 2292&#160;MHz for the downlink to Earth with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> tracking the signal. Data to be transmitted was passed through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convolution_code" title="Convolution code">convolutional encoder</a> so that most communication errors could be <a href="/wiki/Error_detection_and_correction" title="Error detection and correction">corrected</a> by the receiving equipment on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>43</span></sup> The data transmission rate at launch was 256&#160;bit/s, with the rate degrading by about &#8722;1.27&#160;millibit/s for each day during the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd65_8_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-prd65_8-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much of the computation for the mission was performed on Earth and transmitted to the spacecraft, where it was able to retain in memory up to five commands of the 222 possible entries by ground controllers. The spacecraft included two command decoders and a command distribution unit, a very limited form of processor, to direct operations on the spacecraft. This system required that mission operators prepare commands long in advance of transmitting them to the probe. A data storage unit was included to record up to 6,144 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bytes" title="Bytes">bytes</a> of information gathered by the instruments. The digital telemetry unit was used to prepare the collected data in one of the thirteen possible formats before transmitting it back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>38</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_instruments">Scientific instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Scientific instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5">Helium Vector <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometer</a> (<b>HVM</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P50 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="944" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>This instrument measured the fine structure of the interplanetary magnetic field, mapped the Jovian magnetic field, and provided magnetic field measurements to evaluate solar wind interaction with Jupiter. The magnetometer consisted of a helium-filled cell mounted on a 6.6&#8211;m boom to partly isolate the instrument from the spacecraft\'s magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCHVM_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCHVM-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> Edward Smith / JPL</small></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=PN_5001&amp;title=Pioneer_10_Helium_Vector_Magnetometer" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20111029195859/http://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer10/mag/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5">Quadrispherical <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">Plasma</a> Analyzer\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P51b - fx.jpg" data-file-height="742" data-file-width="965" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>Peered through a hole in the large dish-shaped antenna to detect particles of the solar wind originating from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCQPA_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCQPA-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> Aaron Barnes / NASA Ames Research Center (<a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20000520044455/http://arwen.arc.nasa.gov/" rel="nofollow">archived website</a>)</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-21">[21]</a></sup></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=PN_5001&amp;title=Pioneer_10_Quadrispherical_Plasma_Analyser" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20111029195757/http://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer10/plasma/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charged_Particle" title="Charged Particle">Charged Particle</a> Instrument (<b>CPI</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P52a - fx.jpg" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>Detected cosmic rays in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCCPI_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCCPI-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/John_Alexander_Simpson" title="John Alexander Simpson">John Simpson</a> / University of Chicago</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-21">[21]</a></sup></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20151024040753/http://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer10/particle/cpi/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_Ray" title="Cosmic Ray">Cosmic Ray</a> Telescope (<b>CRT</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P52b - fx.jpg" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>Collected data on the composition of the cosmic ray particles and their energy ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCCRT_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCCRT-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/Frank_B._McDonald" title="Frank B. McDonald">Frank B. McDonald</a> / NASA Goddard Space Flight Center</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-21">[21]</a></sup></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search?filter=PN_5001&amp;title=Pioneer_10_Cosmic_Ray_Telescope" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20151024014456/http://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer10/particle/crt/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5"><a href="/wiki/Geiger_tube_telescope" title="Geiger tube telescope">Geiger Tube Telescope</a> (<b>GTT</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - p53 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="861" data-file-width="662" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg/100px-Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg/200px-Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>Surveyed the intensities, energy spectra, and angular distributions of electrons and protons along the spacecraft\'s path through the radiation belts of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCGTT_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCGTT-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_A._Van_Allen" title="James A. Van Allen">James A. Van Allen</a> / University of Iowa (<a class="external text" href="http://www-pw.physics.uiowa.edu/pioneer/home.html" rel="nofollow">website</a>)</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-21">[21]</a></sup></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=PN_5001&amp;title=Pioneer_10_Gieger_Tube_Telescope" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20151024012743/http://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer10/particle/gtt/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5">Trapped <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">Radiation</a> Detector (<b>TRD</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P54 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="949" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>Included an <i>unfocused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerenkov_counter" title="Cerenkov counter">Cerenkov counter</a></i> that detected the light emitted in a particular direction as particles passed through it recording electrons of energy, 0.5 to 12 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">MeV</a>, an <i>electron scatter detector</i> for electrons of energy, 100 to 400 keV, and a <i>minimum ionizing detector</i> consisting of a solid-state diode that measured minimum ionizing particles (&lt;3 MeV)  and protons in the range of 50 to 350 MeV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCTRD_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCTRD-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> R. Fillius / University of California San Diego</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-21">[21]</a></sup></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20151024040800/http://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer10/particle/trd/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5"><a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">Meteoroid</a> Detectors\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P56 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="950" decoding="async" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>Twelve panels of pressurized cell detectors mounted on the back of the main dish antenna recorded penetrating impacts of small meteoroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCMD_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCMD-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> William Kinard / NASA Langley Research Center</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-21">[21]</a></sup></li>\n<li><small><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20121005062209/http://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/datasetSearch.do?spacecraft=Pioneer%2010&amp;experiment=Meteoroid%20Detectors" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive list</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5">Asteroid/Meteoroid Detector (<b>AMD</b>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - These strips were then processed to build up a visual image of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCIPP_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCIPP-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/Tom_Gehrels" title="Tom Gehrels">Tom Gehrels</a> / University of Arizona</small><sup archive list</a></small></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n<hr/>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="background: #e5e5e5">Infrared <a href="/wiki/Radiometer" Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCIRR_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCIRR-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><small><b>Principal investigator:</b> Andrew Ingersoll / California Institute class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Launch and trajectory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Launch_of_Pioneer_10-2.jpg/263px-Launch_of_Pioneer_10-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Launch_of_Pioneer_10-2.jpg/350px-Launch_of_Pioneer_10-2.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The launch of <i>Pioneer 10</i></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:177px;max-width:177px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_mission_jupiter.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pioneer_10_mission_jupiter.svg/175px-Pioneer_10_mission_jupiter.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pioneer_10_mission_jupiter.svg/263px-Pioneer_10_mission_jupiter.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pioneer_10_mission_jupiter.svg/350px-Pioneer_10_mission_jupiter.svg.png 2x" width="175"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Pioneer 10</i> interplanetary trajectory</div></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="210" data-file-width="539" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg/275px-Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg/413px-Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg/550px-Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg.png 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Outersolarsystem-probes-4407b.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map comparing locations and trajectories of the <i>Pioneer 10</i> (blue), <i>Pioneer 11</i> (green), <i>Voyager 2</i> ( red) and <i>Voyager 1</i> (purple) spacecraft, as of 2007</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Pioneer 10</i> was launched on March 3, 1972 at 01:49:00 UTC (March 2 local time) by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_36" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 36">Space Launch Complex 36A</a> in Florida, aboard an Atlas-Centaur launch vehicle. The third stage consisted of a solid fuel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TE364-4" title="TE364-4">TE364-4</a> developed specifically for the Pioneer missions. This stage provided about 15,000 pounds of thrust and spun up the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The spacecraft had an initial spin rate of 30 rpm. Twenty minutes following the launch, the vehicle\'s three booms were extended, which slowed the rotation rate to 4.8 rpm. This rate was maintained throughout the voyage. The launch vehicle accelerated the probe for net interval of 17 minutes, reaching a velocity of 51,682&#160;km/h (32,114&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogers199523_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers199523-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the high-gain antenna was contacted, several of the instruments were activated for testing while the spacecraft was moving through the Earth\'s radiation belts. Ninety minutes after launch, the spacecraft reached interplanetary space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogers199523_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers199523-32">[32]</a></sup> <i>Pioneer 10</i> passed by the Moon in 11 hours<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198073_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198073-33">[33]</a></sup> and became the fastest human-made object at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows199017_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows199017-34">[34]</a></sup> Two days after launch, the scientific instruments were turned on, beginning with the cosmic ray telescope. After ten days, all of the instruments were active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198073_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198073-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the first seven months of the journey, the spacecraft made three course corrections. The on-board instruments underwent checkouts, with the <a href="/wiki/Photometer" title="Photometer">photometers</a> examining Jupiter and the <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">Zodiacal light</a>, and experiment packages being used to measure cosmic rays, magnetic fields and the solar wind. The only anomaly during this interval was the failure of the Canopus sensor, which instead required the spacecraft to maintain its orientation using the two Sun sensors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogers199523_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers199523-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While passing through <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a>, <i>Pioneer 10</i> became the first mission to detect interplanetary atoms of helium. It also observed high-energy ions of aluminum and sodium in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>. The spacecraft recorded important <a href="/wiki/Heliophysics" title="Heliophysics">heliophysics</a> data in early August 1972 by registering a <a href="/wiki/Solar_storm_of_August_1972" title="Solar storm of August 1972">solar shock wave</a> when it was at a distance of 2.2 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knipp_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knipp-35">[35]</a></sup> On July 15, 1972, <i>Pioneer 10</i> was the first spacecraft to enter the asteroid belt, located between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter. The project planners expected a safe passage through the belt, and the closest the trajectory would take the spacecraft to any of the known asteroids was 8,800,000 kilometers (5,500,000&#160;mi). One of the nearest approaches was to the asteroid <a href="/wiki/307_Nike" title="307 Nike">307 Nike</a> on December 2, 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198075_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198075-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The on-board experiments demonstrated a deficiency of particles below a <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometer</a> (&#956;m) in the belt, as compared to the vicinity of the Earth. The density of dust particles between 10&#8211;100&#160;&#956;m did not vary significantly during the trip from the Earth to the outer edge of the belt. Only for particles with a diameter of 100&#160;&#956;m to 1.0&#160;mm did the density show an increase, by a factor of three in the region of the belt. No fragments larger than a millimeter were observed in the belt, indicating these are likely rare; certainly much less common than anticipated. As the spacecraft did not collide with any particles of substantial size, it passed safely through the belt, emerging on the other side about February 15, 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ns57_835_470_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-ns57_835_470-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurgess198232_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurgess198232-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encounter_with_Jupiter">Encounter with Jupiter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Encounter with Jupiter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif/220px-Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif/330px-Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif/440px-Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Pioneer_10_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>Pioneer 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from  3 March 1972 to 31 December 1975<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <i>Pioneer 10</i> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color: Lime ; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a></span></div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_December_4_1973_Jupiter.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="2918" data-file-width="3078" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Pioneer_10_December_4_1973_Jupiter.png/200px-Pioneer_10_December_4_1973_Jupiter.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Pioneer_10_December_4_1973_Jupiter.png/300px-Pioneer_10_December_4_1973_Jupiter.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Pioneer_10_December_4_1973_Jupiter.png/400px-Pioneer_10_December_4_1973_Jupiter.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Pioneer 10\'s</i> trajectory through the Jovian system</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_-_p146.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="411" data-file-width="361" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Pioneer_10_-_p146.jpg/200px-Pioneer_10_-_p146.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Pioneer_10_-_p146.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10_-_p146.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/34/Pioneer_10_-_p146.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Pioneer 10</i> image of Jupiter showing the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> near the right limb</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_-_Ganymede_-_P102a.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="228" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Pioneer_10_-_Ganymede_-_P102a.jpg/200px-Pioneer_10_-_Ganymede_-_P102a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/Pioneer_10_-_Ganymede_-_P102a.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The moon <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> as imaged by <i>Pioneer 10</i></div></div></div></div>\n<p>On November 6, 1973, the <i>Pioneer 10</i> spacecraft was at a distance of 25&#160;million kilometers (16<span style="margin-left:0.2em">&#215;<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>&#160;mi) from Jupiter. Testing of the imaging system began, and the data was successfully received back at the Deep Space Network. A series of 16,000 commands were then uploaded to the spacecraft to control the flyby operations during the next sixty days. The orbit of the outer moon <a href="/wiki/Sinope_(moon)" title="Sinope (moon)">Sinope</a> was crossed on November 8. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a> of Jupiter\'s magnetosphere was reached on November 16, as indicated by a drop in the velocity of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> from 451&#160;km/s (280&#160;mi/s) to 225&#160;km/s (140&#160;mi/s). The <a href="/wiki/Magnetopause" title="Magnetopause">magnetopause</a> was passed through a day later. The spacecraft instruments confirmed that the magnetic field of Jupiter was inverted compared to that of Earth. By the 29th, the orbits of all of the outermost moons had been passed and the spacecraft was operating flawlessly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079&#8211;93_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079&#8211;93-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Red and blue pictures of Jupiter were being generated by the imaging photopolarimeter as the rotation of the spacecraft carried the instrument\'s field of view past the planet. These red and blue colors were combined to produce a synthetic green image, allowing a three-color combination to produce the rendered image. On November 26, a total of twelve such images were received back on Earth. By December 2, the image quality exceeded the best images made from Earth. These were being displayed in real-time back on Earth, and the Pioneer program would later receive an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmy_award" title="Emmy award">Emmy award</a> for this presentation to the media. The motion of the spacecraft produced geometric distortions that later had to be corrected by computer processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079&#8211;93_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079&#8211;93-39">[39]</a></sup> During the encounter, a total of more than 500 images were transmitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980170_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980170-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The trajectory of the spacecraft took it along the magnetic equator of Jupiter, where the <a href="/wiki/Particle_radiation" title="Particle radiation">ion radiation</a> was concentrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198093_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198093-41">[41]</a></sup> Peak <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">flux</a> for this electron radiation is 10,000 times stronger than the maximum radiation around the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980126_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980126-42">[42]</a></sup> Starting on December 3, the radiation around Jupiter caused false commands to be generated. Most of these were corrected by contingency commands, but an image of <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> and a few close ups of Jupiter were lost. Similar false commands would be generated on the way out from the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079&#8211;93_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079&#8211;93-39">[39]</a></sup> Nonetheless, <i>Pioneer 10</i> did succeed in obtaining images of the moons <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>. The image of Ganymede showed low <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> features in the center and near the south pole, while the north pole appeared brighter. Europa was too far away to obtain a detailed image, although some albedo features were apparent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980121_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980121-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The trajectory of <i>Pioneer 10</i> was chosen to take it behind Io, allowing the <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refractive effect</a> of the moon\'s atmosphere on the radio transmissions to be measured. This demonstrated that the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> of the moon was about 700 kilometers (430&#160;mi) above the surface on the day side, and the density ranged from 60,000 electrons per cubic centimeter on the day side, down to 9,000 on the night face. An unexpected discovery was that Io was orbiting within a cloud of hydrogen that extended for about 805,000 kilometers (500,000&#160;mi), with a width and height of 402,000 kilometers (250,000&#160;mi). A smaller, 110,000 kilometers (68,000&#160;mi) cloud was believed to have been detected near Europa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980121_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980121-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It wasn\'t until after <i>Pioneer 10</i> had cleared the asteroid belt that NASA selected a trajectory towards Jupiter that offered the slingshot effect that would send the spacecraft out of the solar system. <i>Pioneer 10</i> was the first spacecraft to attempt such a maneuver and became a proof of concept for the missions that would follow. Such an extended mission was not originally something that was planned, but was planned for prior to launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the closest approach, the velocity of the spacecraft reached 132,000&#160;km/h,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198079-45">[45]</a></sup> and it came within 132,252 kilometers (82,178&#160;mi) of the outer atmosphere of Jupiter. Close-up images of the Great Red Spot and the terminator were obtained. Communication with the spacecraft then ceased as it passed behind the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198093_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198093-41">[41]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Radio_occultation" title="Radio occultation">radio occultation</a> data allowed the temperature structure of the outer atmosphere to be measured, showing a temperature inversion between the altitudes with 10 and 100&#160;mbar pressures. Temperatures at the 10 mbar level ranged from &#8722;133 to &#8722;113&#160;&#176;C (&#8722;207 to &#8722;171&#160;&#176;F), while temperatures at the 100&#160;mbar level were &#8722;183 to &#8722;163&#160;&#176;C (&#8722;297.4 to &#8722;261.4&#160;&#176;F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980135_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980135-46">[46]</a></sup> The spacecraft generated an infrared map of the planet, which confirmed the idea that the planet radiated more heat than it received from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980141_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980141-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crescent images of the planet were then returned as  <i>Pioneer 10</i>  moved away from the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198090_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198090-48">[48]</a></sup> As the spacecraft headed outward, it again passed the bow shock of Jupiter\'s magnetosphere. As this front is constantly shifting in space because of dynamic interaction with the solar wind, the vehicle crossed the bow shock a total of 17 times before it escaped completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980123&#8211;124_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess1980123&#8211;124-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_space">Deep space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Deep space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="649" data-file-width="847" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/220px-Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/330px-Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/440px-Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pioneer 10 and 11 speed and distance from the Sun</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Pioneer 10</i>  crossed the orbit of Saturn in 1976 and the orbit of Uranus in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198091_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFimmelvan_AllenBurgess198091-50">[50]</a></sup> On June 13, 1983, the craft crossed the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, the outermost planet, and so became the first human-made object to leave the proximity of the major planets of the Solar System. The mission came to an official end on March 31, 1997, when it had reached a distance of 67&#160;AU from the Sun, though the spacecraft was still able to transmit coherent data after this date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd65_8_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-prd65_8-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After March 31, 1997, <i>Pioneer 10\'s</i> weak signal continued to be tracked by the Deep Space Network to aid the training of flight controllers in the process of acquiring deep space radio signals. There was an <a href="/wiki/NASA_Institute_for_Advanced_Concepts" title="NASA Institute for Advanced Concepts">Advanced Concepts</a> study applying <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaos theory</a> to extract coherent data from the fading signal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phillips2001_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-phillips2001-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The last successful reception of <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> was received from <i>Pioneer 10</i> on April 27, 2002; subsequent signals were barely strong enough to detect, and provided no usable data. The final, very weak signal from <i>Pioneer 10</i> was received on January 23, 2003 when it was 12 billion kilometers (80 AU) from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Further attempts to contact the spacecraft were unsuccessful. A final attempt was made on the evening of March 4, 2006, the last time the antenna would be correctly aligned with Earth. No response was received from <i>Pioneer 10</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lakdawalla2006_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-lakdawalla2006-53">[53]</a></sup> NASA decided that the RTG units had probably fallen below the power threshold needed to operate the transmitter. Hence, no further attempts at contact were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAngelo2007221_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAngelo2007221-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Timeline of travel\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col" style="width:100px;">Date\n</th>\n<th>Event\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1974-01-01</div>\n</td>\n<td>Begin Pioneer Interstellar Mission\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2" scope="col" style="width:460px;">More\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td scope="col" style="width:100px;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1975-02-10</div>\n</td>\n<td scope="col" style="width:350px;">The US Post Office issued a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_space_exploration_history_on_US_stamps" title="US space exploration history on US stamps">commemorative stamp</a> featuring the <i>Pioneer 10</i> space probe <a href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_Jupiter_1975_Issue-10c.jpg" title="File:Pioneer Jupiter 1975 Issue-10c.jpg"><i>(<b>See image</b>)</i></a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1983-04-25</div>\n</td>\n<td>Crossed orbit of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, still defined as a planet at the time (Pluto\'s irregular orbit meant it was closer to the Sun than Neptune).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1983-06-13</div>\n</td>\n<td>Crossed orbit of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, the furthest planet away from the Sun at the time, to become the first human-made object to depart the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> By dialing 1-900-410-4111, one could access a recording provided by TRW that was made by slowing down and converting <i>Pioneer 10\'s</i> data feed to analog sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1997-03-31</div>\n</td>\n<td>End of mission. Contact is maintained with spacecraft to record telemetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_passes_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager_passes-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1998-02-17</div>\n</td>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> overtakes  <i>Pioneer 10</i>  as the most distant human-made object from the Sun, at 69.419&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_Unit" title="Astronomical Unit">AU</a>. <i>Voyager 1</i> is moving away from the Sun over 1&#160;AU per year faster than <i>Pioneer 10</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_passes_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager_passes-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">2002-03-02</div>\n</td>\n<td>Successful reception of telemetry. 39 minutes of clean data received from a distance of 79.83&#160;AU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoM_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoM-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">2002-04-27</div>\n</td>\n<td>Last successful reception of telemetry. 33 minutes of clean data received from a distance of 80.22 AU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoM_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-EoM-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">2003-01-23</div>\n</td>\n<td>Final signal received from the spacecraft. Reception was very weak and subsequent signals were barely strong enough to detect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoM_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-EoM-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">2003-02-07</div>\n</td>\n<td>Unsuccessful attempt to contact spacecraft<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoM_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-EoM-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">2005-12-30</div>\n</td>\n<td><i>Pioneer 10</i> was projected to be 89.7&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>, traveling at a velocity of 12.51 kilometers/second (28,000 miles/hour), which is approximately 0.000041 the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">2009-10-</div>\n</td>\n<td>Projections indicate that <i>Pioneer 10</i> reached 100&#160;AU. At this point, the spacecraft is approximately 271,000&#160;AU from the nearest star (other than the Sun), <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nearest_star_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nearest_star-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>61&#8211;94</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDSOverview_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDSOverview-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMuller_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-DMuller-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status_and_future">Current status and future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Current status and future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pio10_8feb2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="210" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Pio10_8feb2012.jpg/170px-Pio10_8feb2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Pio10_8feb2012.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pio10_8feb2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Position of <i>Pioneer 10</i> on 8 February 2012</div></div></div>\n<p>On January 1, 2019, <i>Pioneer 10</i> is predicted to be 121.69 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">au</a> from the Earth (about 11.3 billion miles); and traveling at 12.04&#160;km/s (26,900&#160;mph) (relative to the Sun) and traveling outward at about 2.54 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">au</a> per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peat-20120909_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peat-20120909-63">[63]</a></sup> <i>Voyager 2</i> was projected to have passed <i>Pioneer 10</i> by April 2019. Sunlight takes 14.79 hours to reach <i>Pioneer 10</i>. The brightness of the Sun from the spacecraft is magnitude &#8722;16.6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peat-20120909_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peat-20120909-63">[63]</a></sup> <i>Pioneer 10</i> is heading in the direction of the constellation <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">Taurus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peat-20120909_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peat-20120909-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If left undisturbed, <i>Pioneer 10</i> and its sister craft <i>Pioneer 11</i> will join the two Voyager spacecraft and the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> spacecraft in leaving the Solar System to wander the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>. The <i>Pioneer 10</i> trajectory is expected to take it in the general direction of the star <a href="/wiki/Aldebaran" title="Aldebaran">Aldebaran</a>, currently located at a distance of about 68&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a>. If Aldebaran had zero <a href="/wiki/Relative_velocity" title="Relative velocity">relative velocity</a>, it would require more than two million years for the spacecraft to reach it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd65_8_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-prd65_8-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chris_peat_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-chris_peat-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A backup unit, <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_H" title="Pioneer H">Pioneer H</a></i>, is currently on display in the "Milestones of Flight" gallery at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonian-65">[65]</a></sup> Many elements of the mission proved to be critical in the planning of the Voyager program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurrows1990266&#8211;8_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurrows1990266&#8211;8-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneer_plaque">Pioneer plaque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pioneer plaque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_plaque" title="Pioneer plaque">Pioneer plaque</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2246" data-file-width="2856" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg/220px-Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg/330px-Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg/440px-Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer10-plaque_tilt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Pioneer</i> Plaque</div></div></div>\n<p>At the behest of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyer1998302_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyer1998302-9">[9]</a></sup> <i>Pioneer 10</i> and <i>Pioneer 11</i> carry a 152 by 229&#160;mm (6.0 by 9.0&#160;in) gold-anodized aluminum <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_plaque" title="Commemorative plaque">plaque</a> in case either spacecraft is ever found by intelligent life-forms from another planetary system. The plaques feature the nude figures of a human male and female along with several symbols that are designed to provide information about the origin of the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagan1972_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sagan1972-67">[67]</a></sup> The plaque is attached to the antenna support struts to provide some shielding from interstellar dust.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneer_10_in_popular_media"><i>Pioneer 10</i> in popular media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pioneer 10 in popular media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek_V:_The_Final_Frontier" title="Star Trek V: The Final Frontier">Star Trek V: The Final Frontier</a></i>, a Klingon ship destroys <i>Pioneer 10</i> as target practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OkudaOkuda2011_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-OkudaOkuda2011-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the internet multimedia fiction project <i><a href="/wiki/17776" title="17776">17776</a></i>, a sentient <i>Pioneer 10</i> is one of the main characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg/500px-Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div coordinate system">Heliocentric positions</a> of the five <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_probe" title="Interstellar probe">interstellar probes</a> (squares) and other bodies (circles) until 2020, with launch and flyby dates. Markers denote positions on <span class="nowrap">1 January</span> of each year, with every fifth year labelled.<br style="margin-bottom:1ex;"/><span class="nowrap">Plot 1</span> is viewed from the <a associated launches and flybys.</div></div></div>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul
